Hong Kong

Historical Period: Before 1960 (focus on the decade from 1950 to 1959. As background, please briefly mention significant regulations and events from earlier periods, especially those that had a major impact on the circumstances of this decade.)

Researcher Name: Hing Fook-chung

Social Context Includes Three Main Aspects:

  1. Government regulations and legal cases related to religion

    1. Social hostility and actions targeting Christianity

      1. Geopolitical environment and diplomatic relations

          1. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´ „ÄåÊñáÁçª „Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†Áõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

        In the 1950s, the Legislative Council passed incorporation ordinances related to Christian organizations, including:

        • Kowloon Tong Church of the Chinese Christian and Missionary Alliance Incorporation Ordinance, 1950

          • Norwegian Seamen‚Äôs Mission Incorporation Ordinance, 1950

            • Chinese Rhenish Church, Hong Kong Synod, Incorporation Ordinance, 1951

              • Salvation Army Incorporation Ordinance, 1951

                • Church of Christ in China, Wanchai Church, Incorporation Ordinance, 1952

                  • Pentecostal Holiness Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1952

                    • Emmanuel Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1954

                      • Tsimshatsui Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1954

                        • Hong Kong Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                          • Hong Kong Christian Council Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                            • Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                              • Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance, 1956

                                • Kowloon City Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1957

                                  • Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1958

                                    The incorporation ordinances granted legal status to Christian churches and organizations, particularly addressing issues related to the ownership and disposal of land. Churches or organizations incorporated in the 1950s can be categorized into three groups:

                                    1. Churches adapting to the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù movement in China:

                                      After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China in 1949, Chinese Christianity began to follow the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù path to sever ties with imperialism. Denominations that had been active in Hong Kong since the 19th century, whose organizational structures were part of South China or Guangdong, faced the need to establish legal independence based in Hong Kong due to the separation from their counterparts in Mainland China. Examples include the Chinese Rhenish Church, Hong Kong Synod, Incorporation Ordinance (1951), Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance (1956), and Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance (1958).

                                      1. Local churches established before 1950:

                                        Examples include the Kowloon Tong Church of the Chinese Christian and Missionary Alliance Incorporation Ordinance (1950), Norwegian Seamen‚Äôs Mission Incorporation Ordinance (1950), Church of Christ in China, Wanchai Church, Incorporation Ordinance (1952), Pentecostal Holiness Church Incorporation Ordinance (1952), Emmanuel Church Incorporation Ordinance (1954), Tsimshatsui Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1954), Hong Kong Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1955), and Kowloon City Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1957).

                                        1. Newly established organizations after 1950:

                                          Examples include the Hong Kong Christian Council Incorporation Ordinance (1955) and the Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission Incorporation Ordinance (1955).

                                          „Ää‰∫îÊó¨ÁØÄËÅñÊΩîÊúÉÊ≥ïÂúòÊ¢ù‰æã„ÄãPentecostal Holiness Church Incorporation, 1952

                                          „ÄäÈùàÂÖâÂ†ÇÊ≥ïÂúòÊ¢ù‰æã„ÄãEmmanuel Church Incorporation, 1954

                                          1. Criticism of Evangelist Chao Shih-kuang by Leftist Newspapers

                                            In the 1950s, there were no significant incidents of hostility toward Christianity in Hong Kong. However, individual Christian figures were targeted, particularly evangelist Chao Shih-kuang, who arrived in Hong Kong from Shanghai in 1949. Chao had founded the Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission in Shanghai in 1946. Following the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China on October 1, 1949, he and his family left Shanghai, traveling via Zhoushan to Taipei and later flying to Hong Kong. In 1951, as part of the Chinese Communist Party‚Äôs ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù movement, denunciation meetings were held to criticize the ties between Christianity and imperialism. During a denunciation meeting at Shanghai‚Äôs Ling Liang Church in May, Chao became the primary target (Hing 2012: 98-99).

                                            On June 10, 1951, the leftist Hong Kong newspaper Ta Kung Pao published a front-page article titled ‚ÄúChao Shih-kuang, Who Fled from Shanghai to Hong Kong, Slanders the Motherland‚Äù. The report detailed the accusations made against Chao in Shanghai, claiming that he was spreading ‚Äúabsurd and reactionary propaganda‚Äù in Hong Kong and accusing him of slandering the patriotic Three-Self Reform movement of Chinese Christians (Ta Kung Pao 1951a). Other local newspapers also criticized Chao for his close ties with the Kuomintang government (Hsu 1966: 135-136). Concerned for his safety, Chao and his family moved to Indonesia in July 1952 and later relocated to the United States (Hing 2004: 116-117).

                                            During the Chinese church‚Äôs denunciation campaigns in 1951-1952, Hong Kong‚Äôs leftist newspapers also reported on related events. For instance, in April 1951, Ta Kung Pao reported on the accusations against missionaries such as Francis W. Price in Shanghai (Ta Kung Pao 1951b). In June, it covered the Shanghai Christian denunciation meeting, during which the Methodist Church expelled Chiang Kai-shek and Soong Mei-ling from their membership (Ta Kung Pao 1951c).

                                            ‚Äî

                                            1. Controversy Over Renting Theaters for Worship by Southbound Evangelists

                                              Within the Christian community, debates arose over innovative evangelistic methods. In the early 1950s, evangelists arriving from China, such as Ji Chih-wen and Chao Shih-kuang, rented theaters for gatherings and evangelism. For example, Ji Chih-wen (China Evangelistic Mission) held Mandarin-language meetings at Jordan‚Äôs Happy Theatre, attracting nearly 1,000 attendees each time. Similarly, Chao conducted Sunday worship services at Central‚Äôs Entertainment Theatre, filling all 1,200 seats.

                                              This novel approach of using theaters for religious gatherings was not well-received by Hong Kong‚Äôs mainstream churches. In February 1950, the Hong Kong Christian Council issued a notice titled ‚ÄúAdvice to Believers to Attend Meetings Cautiously‚Äù. The notice criticized the use of theaters for Sunday worship, which included rituals such as responsive readings, choir performances, and offerings, akin to formal church services. The council argued that such gatherings, which should promote reverence and solemnity, were inappropriate for theaters, which were seen as ‚Äúworldly‚Äù venues unsuitable for ‚Äúsacred‚Äù religious worship (Hong Kong Christian Council 1950: 6-7). This controversy highlighted the tension between the innovative methods of newly arrived evangelists and the traditional practices of the established church, which resisted the use of secular spaces for sacred purposes.

                                              ‚Äî

                                              1. Division in the Baptist Youth Fellowship (Hing 2004: 166-167)

                                                The Baptist Youth Fellowship Union was a joint organization of various Baptist youth fellowships in Hong Kong, with officers elected by the fellowships. The union operated independently, not under the Baptist Convention or any specific Baptist church. Seasonal meetings were held quarterly, and annual summer camps attracted 300-400 participants, primarily from the Caine Road, Tsim Sha Tsui, and Kowloon City churches.

                                                In 1951, the union held a summer camp in Cheung Chau, with Huo En-de and Chao Chun-ying as speakers. Over 80 people attended, and on the final night, many young participants were deeply moved, confessing their sins and praying throughout the night. After this spiritual revival, the youths experienced significant changes in their lives, emphasizing:

                                                1. The doctrine of rebirth.

                                                  1. Pursuing holy living.

                                                    1. Studying and learning the Word of God through the Bible.

                                                      1. Actively spreading the gospel.

                                                        1. Enthusiastically serving in the church.

                                                          However, these changes led to dissatisfaction with the existing Baptist church structure, and church leaders found the youth increasingly ‚Äúdisobedient.‚Äù This tension ultimately resulted in the separation of the evangelical movement from Baptist churches in Caine Road, Kowloon City, and Shau Kei Wan during the 1960s.

                                                          ‚Äî

                                                          1. Dialogue Between Christianity and Chinese Religious Culture (Ying 2015)

                                                            In 1957, the Christian Study Centre on Chinese Religion and Culture (later renamed the Christian Cultural Study Centre) was established as an interdenominational organization with a mission to promote indigenization. The center‚Äôs founding was closely related to the setbacks faced by Christian missionary efforts in China during the 1950s. Some missionaries in Hong Kong recognized that one of the greatest lessons from the history of Christianity in China was its failure to understand traditional Chinese culture and religion.

                                                            Under the leadership of Bishop R. O. Hall, a group of missionaries initiated the establishment of the center to reflect on these issues. Bishop Hall argued that a significant flaw in Western Christianity was its separation of religious faith from culture. Among the three million Chinese people in Hong Kong, most adhered to traditional cultural and religious practices. Chinese Christians often lost connection with their country‚Äôs traditional way of life after conversion. Thus, the center‚Äôs mission was to study God‚Äôs self-revelation among the Chinese people and their responses to Him, aiming to assist those spreading the gospel in China.

                                                            The World Council of Churches also sought to establish regional research centers worldwide to study Christianity in different cultural contexts and reflect on the church‚Äôs relationship with the world. With funding from the World Council of Churches and the International Missionary Council, the Hong Kong center focused on studying contemporary non-Christian beliefs in China, understanding their vitality, and relating these findings to the practical life of Chinese churches. Activities included publishing the English quarterly Quarterly Notes, the Chinese magazine Ching Fung, and organizing research meetings and seminars.

                                                            Key participants included Western missionaries like Paul Jefferies (Methodist Church), Andrew T. Roy (American Presbyterian Mission), R. P. Kramers (Netherlands Missionary Council), Loren Noren (American Baptist), and Gerhard Reichlt (Tao Fong Shan). Chinese contributors included Li Siu-keung, Hu Zan-yun, Zhou Yi-fu, Hsieh Fu-ya, Ho Shi-ming, Kung Tin-man, and Fung Shang-lee. Through their collaborative efforts, ‚Äúindigenization‚Äù or ‚Äúcontextual theology‚Äù became a central focus of the center during the 1950s and 1960s.

      1. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´„ÄåÊñáÁçª „Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†Áõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

    Geopolitical Factors Influencing Hong Kong in the 1950s

    The new geopolitical landscape in East Asia during the Cold War profoundly impacted Hong Kong in the 1950s. This period was shaped by two major events: the division between the newly established Chinese Communist regime on the mainland and the Kuomintang (KMT) government in Taiwan following the end of the Chinese Civil War, and the outbreak of the Korean War. These two developments were interrelated, significantly influencing Hong Kong‚Äôs trajectory (Mark 2004; Roberts & Carroll 2016).

    With the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, a massive influx of Chinese refugees poured into Hong Kong. By 1950, Hong Kong‚Äôs population reached 2.36 million and rose to 2.5 million by 1956, with an estimated one million being refugees from China. In 1955, the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR) published The Problem of Chinese Refugees in Hong Kong, providing a comprehensive account of the situation and the challenges faced by Chinese refugees. The 1956 Hong Kong government annual report identified education, healthcare, housing, public security, and sanitation as the main social issues resulting from the surge in population (Hing 2002: 33-44; Hing 2004: 81-87).

    The PRC‚Äôs Policy on Hong Kong and Strategic Utilization

    After the founding of the PRC, the colonial future of Hong Kong attracted close attention. Many missionary societies speculated that the Chinese Communist Party (CCP), driven by nationalist considerations, might ‚Äúliberate‚Äù Hong Kong. To avoid potential losses, these societies were hesitant to invest resources in the territory. However, in 1954, Premier Zhou Enlai clarified China‚Äôs policy on Hong Kong, stating that ‚Äúthe time was not yet ripe‚Äù to reclaim it.

    During the Korean War, the CCP‚Äôs involvement led to a UN embargo on strategic goods and a complete U.S. trade embargo on China. Hong Kong emerged as a vital hub for smuggling goods into China and facilitating remittance flows from overseas Chinese. In 1957, Zhou reaffirmed Hong Kong‚Äôs role as an economic bridge between China and the outside world. Under the policy of ‚Äúlong-term planning and full utilization,‚Äù Hong Kong retained its colonial status and assumed a unique role amid the Cold War, earning the nickname ‚Äúthe Berlin of the East‚Äù (Carroll 2007: 140-148).

    Balancing Between the CCP and KMT

    Hong Kong sought to maintain a delicate balance in the face of the rivalry between the CCP and KMT. Although the British government was allied with the United States, it avoided turning Hong Kong into an anti-communist stronghold. Measures to limit CCP activities in Hong Kong were also applied to pro-KMT forces. Britain aimed to preserve Hong Kong‚Äôs status as a free-trade port without entangling it in the political struggle between the two parties.

    Nevertheless, this neutrality could not prevent the presence of CCP and KMT influence. Political factions emerged in industries, media, cultural institutions, neighborhood organizations, unions, chambers of commerce, and schools, divided into pro-KMT ‚Äúrightists‚Äù and pro-CCP ‚Äúleftists.‚Äù Despite efforts to prevent political propaganda in schools, some institutions still aligned with either the KMT or CCP. Meanwhile, the U.S. sought to strengthen anti-communist propaganda, promoting democracy and freedom among Chinese refugees. During this period, Hong Kong became an important cultural Cold War battleground and a middle ground for CCP and KMT forces (Lo 2020: 29-40, 98; Lui 2014: 1-12).

    The colonial government‚Äôs support for Christian schools in the 1950s and 1960s was partly motivated by a desire to counter communist ideology (Leung & Chan 2003: 23-28).

    Refugees and Missionary Efforts

    The influx of Chinese refugees reinforced Hong Kong‚Äôs character as a migrant-refugee society. Pro-CCP factions praised the ‚ÄúRed Motherland,‚Äù while pro-KMT factions condemned the CCP as ‚ÄúRed Bandits.‚Äù Some ‚ÄúSouthbound Literati,‚Äù nostalgic for ‚Äúcultural China,‚Äù created diverse imaginations of China in 1950s Hong Kong.

    Among the refugee influx were Western missionaries fleeing China. Some initially attempted to remain under the communist regime. In June 1950, 1,900 Protestant missionaries were still in China, a significant decrease from over 4,000 after the war. However, uncertain prospects forced many to leave. By July 1950, the number of Protestant missionaries had dropped to 1,200. As China‚Äôs involvement in the Korean War escalated, the CCP launched the ‚ÄúResist America, Aid Korea‚Äù campaign, which heightened anti-American sentiment and targeted missionaries. In February 1951, even the China Inland Mission (CIM), which had long resisted withdrawal, decided to fully evacuate its personnel. Other missionary organizations followed suit, demanding complete withdrawal of missionaries from China (Hing 2012: 262-281).

    Concurrently, the CCP launched the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform Movement,‚Äù aimed at creating self-governing, self-supporting, and self-propagating churches while severing ties with imperialism and purging ‚ÄúAmerican imperialist toxins.‚Äù This accelerated the CCP‚Äôs control over and transformation of Chinese churches (Hing 2012: 21-76).

    Hong Kong as a Refugee Hub and Missionary Base

    Amid the ‚ÄúGreat Exodus,‚Äù British Hong Kong, located on the southern frontier of China, became the first stop for departing missionaries. Many missionaries, witnessing the plight of refugees in Hong Kong, began working with them even without approval from their missionary societies. Rennie‚Äôs Mill (Tiu Keng Leng) became the largest refugee camp, where missionaries from organizations like the Norwegian Lutheran Mission, Lutheran Church‚ÄìMissouri Synod, Christian and Missionary Alliance, American Evangelical and Reformed Church, Swedish Free Mission, and Mission Covenant Church of Norway served. These missionaries established churches, schools, medical services, relief programs, and seminaries within the refugee camps (Leung 2000; Yang 2014).

    Under the guise of aiding Chinese refugees, significant missionary resources poured into Hong Kong.

          1. Ë´ãÂú®ÊñáÊ™îÊú´ „ÄåÊñáÁçª „Äç ËôïÊ∑ªÂä†Áõ∏ÈóúÊñáÁçªÁöÑÂá∫ËôïÔºàËá≥Â∞ë‰∏ÄËôïÂá∫ËôïÔºåË∂äÂ§öË∂äÂ•Ω„ÄÇ Â¶ÇÂèØËÉΩÔºå‰πüË´ãÂåÖÊã¨‰∏≠Êó•ÈüìËã±ÊàñÂÖ∂‰ªñÊ≠êÊ¥≤Ë™ûË®ÄÁöÑÊñáÁçªÂá∫ËôïÔºâ„ÄÇ ‚òê

        In the 1950s, the Legislative Council passed incorporation ordinances related to Christian organizations, including:

        • Kowloon Tong Church of the Chinese Christian and Missionary Alliance Incorporation Ordinance, 1950

          • Norwegian Seamen‚Äôs Mission Incorporation Ordinance, 1950

            • Chinese Rhenish Church, Hong Kong Synod, Incorporation Ordinance, 1951

              • Salvation Army Incorporation Ordinance, 1951

                • Church of Christ in China, Wanchai Church, Incorporation Ordinance, 1952

                  • Pentecostal Holiness Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1952

                    • Emmanuel Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1954

                      • Tsimshatsui Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1954

                        • Hong Kong Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                          • Hong Kong Christian Council Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                            • Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                              • Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance, 1956

                                • Kowloon City Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1957

                                  • Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1958

                                    The incorporation ordinances granted legal status to Christian churches and organizations, particularly addressing issues related to the ownership and disposal of land. Churches or organizations incorporated in the 1950s can be categorized into three groups:

                                    1. Churches adapting to the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù movement in China:

                                      After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China in 1949, Chinese Christianity began to follow the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù path to sever ties with imperialism. Denominations that had been active in Hong Kong since the 19th century, whose organizational structures were part of South China or Guangdong, faced the need to establish legal independence based in Hong Kong due to the separation from their counterparts in Mainland China. Examples include the Chinese Rhenish Church, Hong Kong Synod, Incorporation Ordinance (1951), Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance (1956), and Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance (1958).

                                      1. Local churches established before 1950:

                                        Examples include the Kowloon Tong Church of the Chinese Christian and Missionary Alliance Incorporation Ordinance (1950), Norwegian Seamen‚Äôs Mission Incorporation Ordinance (1950), Church of Christ in China, Wanchai Church, Incorporation Ordinance (1952), Pentecostal Holiness Church Incorporation Ordinance (1952), Emmanuel Church Incorporation Ordinance (1954), Tsimshatsui Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1954), Hong Kong Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1955), and Kowloon City Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1957).

                                        1. Newly established organizations after 1950:

                                          Examples include the Hong Kong Christian Council Incorporation Ordinance (1955) and the Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission Incorporation Ordinance (1955).

                                          1. Criticism of Evangelist Chao Shih-kuang by Leftist Newspapers

                                            In the 1950s, there were no significant incidents of hostility toward Christianity in Hong Kong. However, individual Christian figures were targeted, particularly evangelist Chao Shih-kuang, who arrived in Hong Kong from Shanghai in 1949. Chao had founded the Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission in Shanghai in 1946. Following the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China on October 1, 1949, he and his family left Shanghai, traveling via Zhoushan to Taipei and later flying to Hong Kong. In 1951, as part of the Chinese Communist Party‚Äôs ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù movement, denunciation meetings were held to criticize the ties between Christianity and imperialism. During a denunciation meeting at Shanghai‚Äôs Ling Liang Church in May, Chao became the primary target (Hing 2012: 98-99).

                                            On June 10, 1951, the leftist Hong Kong newspaper Ta Kung Pao published a front-page article titled ‚ÄúChao Shih-kuang, Who Fled from Shanghai to Hong Kong, Slanders the Motherland‚Äù. The report detailed the accusations made against Chao in Shanghai, claiming that he was spreading ‚Äúabsurd and reactionary propaganda‚Äù in Hong Kong and accusing him of slandering the patriotic Three-Self Reform movement of Chinese Christians (Ta Kung Pao 1951a). Other local newspapers also criticized Chao for his close ties with the Kuomintang government (Hsu 1966: 135-136). Concerned for his safety, Chao and his family moved to Indonesia in July 1952 and later relocated to the United States (Hing 2004: 116-117).

                                            During the Chinese church‚Äôs denunciation campaigns in 1951-1952, Hong Kong‚Äôs leftist newspapers also reported on related events. For instance, in April 1951, Ta Kung Pao reported on the accusations against missionaries such as Francis W. Price in Shanghai (Ta Kung Pao 1951b). In June, it covered the Shanghai Christian denunciation meeting, during which the Methodist Church expelled Chiang Kai-shek and Soong Mei-ling from their membership (Ta Kung Pao 1951c).

                                            ‚Äî

                                            1. Controversy Over Renting Theaters for Worship by Southbound Evangelists

                                              Within the Christian community, debates arose over innovative evangelistic methods. In the early 1950s, evangelists arriving from China, such as Ji Chih-wen and Chao Shih-kuang, rented theaters for gatherings and evangelism. For example, Ji Chih-wen (China Evangelistic Mission) held Mandarin-language meetings at Jordan‚Äôs Happy Theatre, attracting nearly 1,000 attendees each time. Similarly, Chao conducted Sunday worship services at Central‚Äôs Entertainment Theatre, filling all 1,200 seats.

                                              This novel approach of using theaters for religious gatherings was not well-received by Hong Kong‚Äôs mainstream churches. In February 1950, the Hong Kong Christian Council issued a notice titled ‚ÄúAdvice to Believers to Attend Meetings Cautiously‚Äù. The notice criticized the use of theaters for Sunday worship, which included rituals such as responsive readings, choir performances, and offerings, akin to formal church services. The council argued that such gatherings, which should promote reverence and solemnity, were inappropriate for theaters, which were seen as ‚Äúworldly‚Äù venues unsuitable for ‚Äúsacred‚Äù religious worship (Hong Kong Christian Council 1950: 6-7). This controversy highlighted the tension between the innovative methods of newly arrived evangelists and the traditional practices of the established church, which resisted the use of secular spaces for sacred purposes.

                                              ‚Äî

                                              1. Division in the Baptist Youth Fellowship (Hing 2004: 166-167)

                                                The Baptist Youth Fellowship Union was a joint organization of various Baptist youth fellowships in Hong Kong, with officers elected by the fellowships. The union operated independently, not under the Baptist Convention or any specific Baptist church. Seasonal meetings were held quarterly, and annual summer camps attracted 300-400 participants, primarily from the Caine Road, Tsim Sha Tsui, and Kowloon City churches.

                                                In 1951, the union held a summer camp in Cheung Chau, with Huo En-de and Chao Chun-ying as speakers. Over 80 people attended, and on the final night, many young participants were deeply moved, confessing their sins and praying throughout the night. After this spiritual revival, the youths experienced significant changes in their lives, emphasizing:

                                                1. The doctrine of rebirth.

                                                  1. Pursuing holy living.

                                                    1. Studying and learning the Word of God through the Bible.

                                                      1. Actively spreading the gospel.

                                                        1. Enthusiastically serving in the church.

                                                          However, these changes led to dissatisfaction with the existing Baptist church structure, and church leaders found the youth increasingly ‚Äúdisobedient.‚Äù This tension ultimately resulted in the separation of the evangelical movement from Baptist churches in Caine Road, Kowloon City, and Shau Kei Wan during the 1960s.

                                                          ‚Äî

                                                          1. Dialogue Between Christianity and Chinese Religious Culture (Ying 2015)

                                                            In 1957, the Christian Study Centre on Chinese Religion and Culture (later renamed the Christian Cultural Study Centre) was established as an interdenominational organization with a mission to promote indigenization. The center‚Äôs founding was closely related to the setbacks faced by Christian missionary efforts in China during the 1950s. Some missionaries in Hong Kong recognized that one of the greatest lessons from the history of Christianity in China was its failure to understand traditional Chinese culture and religion.

                                                            Under the leadership of Bishop R. O. Hall, a group of missionaries initiated the establishment of the center to reflect on these issues. Bishop Hall argued that a significant flaw in Western Christianity was its separation of religious faith from culture. Among the three million Chinese people in Hong Kong, most adhered to traditional cultural and religious practices. Chinese Christians often lost connection with their country‚Äôs traditional way of life after conversion. Thus, the center‚Äôs mission was to study God‚Äôs self-revelation among the Chinese people and their responses to Him, aiming to assist those spreading the gospel in China.

                                                            The World Council of Churches also sought to establish regional research centers worldwide to study Christianity in different cultural contexts and reflect on the church‚Äôs relationship with the world. With funding from the World Council of Churches and the International Missionary Council, the Hong Kong center focused on studying contemporary non-Christian beliefs in China, understanding their vitality, and relating these findings to the practical life of Chinese churches. Activities included publishing the English quarterly Quarterly Notes, the Chinese magazine Ching Fung, and organizing research meetings and seminars.

                                                            Key participants included Western missionaries like Paul Jefferies (Methodist Church), Andrew T. Roy (American Presbyterian Mission), R. P. Kramers (Netherlands Missionary Council), Loren Noren (American Baptist), and Gerhard Reichlt (Tao Fong Shan). Chinese contributors included Li Siu-keung, Hu Zan-yun, Zhou Yi-fu, Hsieh Fu-ya, Ho Shi-ming, Kung Tin-man, and Fung Shang-lee. Through their collaborative efforts, ‚Äúindigenization‚Äù or ‚Äúcontextual theology‚Äù became a central focus of the center during the 1950s and 1960s.

    Geopolitical Factors Influencing Hong Kong in the 1950s

    The new geopolitical landscape in East Asia during the Cold War profoundly impacted Hong Kong in the 1950s. This period was shaped by two major events: the division between the newly established Chinese Communist regime on the mainland and the Kuomintang (KMT) government in Taiwan following the end of the Chinese Civil War, and the outbreak of the Korean War. These two developments were interrelated, significantly influencing Hong Kong‚Äôs trajectory (Mark 2004; Roberts & Carroll 2016).

    With the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, a massive influx of Chinese refugees poured into Hong Kong. By 1950, Hong Kong‚Äôs population reached 2.36 million and rose to 2.5 million by 1956, with an estimated one million being refugees from China. In 1955, the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR) published The Problem of Chinese Refugees in Hong Kong, providing a comprehensive account of the situation and the challenges faced by Chinese refugees. The 1956 Hong Kong government annual report identified education, healthcare, housing, public security, and sanitation as the main social issues resulting from the surge in population (Hing 2002: 33-44; Hing 2004: 81-87).

    The PRC‚Äôs Policy on Hong Kong and Strategic Utilization

    After the founding of the PRC, the colonial future of Hong Kong attracted close attention. Many missionary societies speculated that the Chinese Communist Party (CCP), driven by nationalist considerations, might ‚Äúliberate‚Äù Hong Kong. To avoid potential losses, these societies were hesitant to invest resources in the territory. However, in 1954, Premier Zhou Enlai clarified China‚Äôs policy on Hong Kong, stating that ‚Äúthe time was not yet ripe‚Äù to reclaim it.

    During the Korean War, the CCP‚Äôs involvement led to a UN embargo on strategic goods and a complete U.S. trade embargo on China. Hong Kong emerged as a vital hub for smuggling goods into China and facilitating remittance flows from overseas Chinese. In 1957, Zhou reaffirmed Hong Kong‚Äôs role as an economic bridge between China and the outside world. Under the policy of ‚Äúlong-term planning and full utilization,‚Äù Hong Kong retained its colonial status and assumed a unique role amid the Cold War, earning the nickname ‚Äúthe Berlin of the East‚Äù (Carroll 2007: 140-148).

    Balancing Between the CCP and KMT

    Hong Kong sought to maintain a delicate balance in the face of the rivalry between the CCP and KMT. Although the British government was allied with the United States, it avoided turning Hong Kong into an anti-communist stronghold. Measures to limit CCP activities in Hong Kong were also applied to pro-KMT forces. Britain aimed to preserve Hong Kong‚Äôs status as a free-trade port without entangling it in the political struggle between the two parties.

    Nevertheless, this neutrality could not prevent the presence of CCP and KMT influence. Political factions emerged in industries, media, cultural institutions, neighborhood organizations, unions, chambers of commerce, and schools, divided into pro-KMT ‚Äúrightists‚Äù and pro-CCP ‚Äúleftists.‚Äù Despite efforts to prevent political propaganda in schools, some institutions still aligned with either the KMT or CCP. Meanwhile, the U.S. sought to strengthen anti-communist propaganda, promoting democracy and freedom among Chinese refugees. During this period, Hong Kong became an important cultural Cold War battleground and a middle ground for CCP and KMT forces (Lo 2020: 29-40, 98; Lui 2014: 1-12).

    The colonial government‚Äôs support for Christian schools in the 1950s and 1960s was partly motivated by a desire to counter communist ideology (Leung & Chan 2003: 23-28).

    Refugees and Missionary Efforts

    The influx of Chinese refugees reinforced Hong Kong‚Äôs character as a migrant-refugee society. Pro-CCP factions praised the ‚ÄúRed Motherland,‚Äù while pro-KMT factions condemned the CCP as ‚ÄúRed Bandits.‚Äù Some ‚ÄúSouthbound Literati,‚Äù nostalgic for ‚Äúcultural China,‚Äù created diverse imaginations of China in 1950s Hong Kong.

    Among the refugee influx were Western missionaries fleeing China. Some initially attempted to remain under the communist regime. In June 1950, 1,900 Protestant missionaries were still in China, a significant decrease from over 4,000 after the war. However, uncertain prospects forced many to leave. By July 1950, the number of Protestant missionaries had dropped to 1,200. As China‚Äôs involvement in the Korean War escalated, the CCP launched the ‚ÄúResist America, Aid Korea‚Äù campaign, which heightened anti-American sentiment and targeted missionaries. In February 1951, even the China Inland Mission (CIM), which had long resisted withdrawal, decided to fully evacuate its personnel. Other missionary organizations followed suit, demanding complete withdrawal of missionaries from China (Hing 2012: 262-281).

    Concurrently, the CCP launched the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform Movement,‚Äù aimed at creating self-governing, self-supporting, and self-propagating churches while severing ties with imperialism and purging ‚ÄúAmerican imperialist toxins.‚Äù This accelerated the CCP‚Äôs control over and transformation of Chinese churches (Hing 2012: 21-76).

    Hong Kong as a Refugee Hub and Missionary Base

    Amid the ‚ÄúGreat Exodus,‚Äù British Hong Kong, located on the southern frontier of China, became the first stop for departing missionaries. Many missionaries, witnessing the plight of refugees in Hong Kong, began working with them even without approval from their missionary societies. Rennie‚Äôs Mill (Tiu Keng Leng) became the largest refugee camp, where missionaries from organizations like the Norwegian Lutheran Mission, Lutheran Church‚ÄìMissouri Synod, Christian and Missionary Alliance, American Evangelical and Reformed Church, Swedish Free Mission, and Mission Covenant Church of Norway served. These missionaries established churches, schools, medical services, relief programs, and seminaries within the refugee camps (Leung 2000; Yang 2014).

    Under the guise of aiding Chinese refugees, significant missionary resources poured into Hong Kong.

        In the 1950s, the Legislative Council passed incorporation ordinances related to Christian organizations, including:

        • Kowloon Tong Church of the Chinese Christian and Missionary Alliance Incorporation Ordinance, 1950

          • Norwegian Seamen‚Äôs Mission Incorporation Ordinance, 1950

            • Chinese Rhenish Church, Hong Kong Synod, Incorporation Ordinance, 1951

              • Salvation Army Incorporation Ordinance, 1951

                • Church of Christ in China, Wanchai Church, Incorporation Ordinance, 1952

                  • Pentecostal Holiness Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1952

                    • Emmanuel Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1954

                      • Tsimshatsui Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1954

                        • Hong Kong Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                          • Hong Kong Christian Council Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                            • Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                              • Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance, 1956

                                • Kowloon City Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1957

                                  • Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1958

                                    The incorporation ordinances granted legal status to Christian churches and organizations, particularly addressing issues related to the ownership and disposal of land. Churches or organizations incorporated in the 1950s can be categorized into three groups:

                                    1. Churches adapting to the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù movement in China:

                                      After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China in 1949, Chinese Christianity began to follow the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù path to sever ties with imperialism. Denominations that had been active in Hong Kong since the 19th century, whose organizational structures were part of South China or Guangdong, faced the need to establish legal independence based in Hong Kong due to the separation from their counterparts in Mainland China. Examples include the Chinese Rhenish Church, Hong Kong Synod, Incorporation Ordinance (1951), Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance (1956), and Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance (1958).

                                      1. Local churches established before 1950:

                                        Examples include the Kowloon Tong Church of the Chinese Christian and Missionary Alliance Incorporation Ordinance (1950), Norwegian Seamen‚Äôs Mission Incorporation Ordinance (1950), Church of Christ in China, Wanchai Church, Incorporation Ordinance (1952), Pentecostal Holiness Church Incorporation Ordinance (1952), Emmanuel Church Incorporation Ordinance (1954), Tsimshatsui Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1954), Hong Kong Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1955), and Kowloon City Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1957).

                                        1. Newly established organizations after 1950:

                                          Examples include the Hong Kong Christian Council Incorporation Ordinance (1955) and the Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission Incorporation Ordinance (1955).

                                          ÊÇ®ÁöÑÂõûÁ≠îÔºö

                                          1. Criticism of Evangelist Chao Shih-kuang by Leftist Newspapers

                                            In the 1950s, there were no significant incidents of hostility toward Christianity in Hong Kong. However, individual Christian figures were targeted, particularly evangelist Chao Shih-kuang, who arrived in Hong Kong from Shanghai in 1949. Chao had founded the Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission in Shanghai in 1946. Following the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China on October 1, 1949, he and his family left Shanghai, traveling via Zhoushan to Taipei and later flying to Hong Kong. In 1951, as part of the Chinese Communist Party‚Äôs ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù movement, denunciation meetings were held to criticize the ties between Christianity and imperialism. During a denunciation meeting at Shanghai‚Äôs Ling Liang Church in May, Chao became the primary target (Hing 2012: 98-99).

                                            On June 10, 1951, the leftist Hong Kong newspaper Ta Kung Pao published a front-page article titled ‚ÄúChao Shih-kuang, Who Fled from Shanghai to Hong Kong, Slanders the Motherland‚Äù. The report detailed the accusations made against Chao in Shanghai, claiming that he was spreading ‚Äúabsurd and reactionary propaganda‚Äù in Hong Kong and accusing him of slandering the patriotic Three-Self Reform movement of Chinese Christians (Ta Kung Pao 1951a). Other local newspapers also criticized Chao for his close ties with the Kuomintang government (Hsu 1966: 135-136). Concerned for his safety, Chao and his family moved to Indonesia in July 1952 and later relocated to the United States (Hing 2004: 116-117).

                                            During the Chinese church‚Äôs denunciation campaigns in 1951-1952, Hong Kong‚Äôs leftist newspapers also reported on related events. For instance, in April 1951, Ta Kung Pao reported on the accusations against missionaries such as Francis W. Price in Shanghai (Ta Kung Pao 1951b). In June, it covered the Shanghai Christian denunciation meeting, during which the Methodist Church expelled Chiang Kai-shek and Soong Mei-ling from their membership (Ta Kung Pao 1951c).

                                            ‚Äî

                                            1. Controversy Over Renting Theaters for Worship by Southbound Evangelists

                                              Within the Christian community, debates arose over innovative evangelistic methods. In the early 1950s, evangelists arriving from China, such as Ji Chih-wen and Chao Shih-kuang, rented theaters for gatherings and evangelism. For example, Ji Chih-wen (China Evangelistic Mission) held Mandarin-language meetings at Jordan‚Äôs Happy Theatre, attracting nearly 1,000 attendees each time. Similarly, Chao conducted Sunday worship services at Central‚Äôs Entertainment Theatre, filling all 1,200 seats.

                                              This novel approach of using theaters for religious gatherings was not well-received by Hong Kong‚Äôs mainstream churches. In February 1950, the Hong Kong Christian Council issued a notice titled ‚ÄúAdvice to Believers to Attend Meetings Cautiously‚Äù. The notice criticized the use of theaters for Sunday worship, which included rituals such as responsive readings, choir performances, and offerings, akin to formal church services. The council argued that such gatherings, which should promote reverence and solemnity, were inappropriate for theaters, which were seen as ‚Äúworldly‚Äù venues unsuitable for ‚Äúsacred‚Äù religious worship (Hong Kong Christian Council 1950: 6-7). This controversy highlighted the tension between the innovative methods of newly arrived evangelists and the traditional practices of the established church, which resisted the use of secular spaces for sacred purposes.

                                              ‚Äî

                                              1. Division in the Baptist Youth Fellowship (Hing 2004: 166-167)

                                                The Baptist Youth Fellowship Union was a joint organization of various Baptist youth fellowships in Hong Kong, with officers elected by the fellowships. The union operated independently, not under the Baptist Convention or any specific Baptist church. Seasonal meetings were held quarterly, and annual summer camps attracted 300-400 participants, primarily from the Caine Road, Tsim Sha Tsui, and Kowloon City churches.

                                                In 1951, the union held a summer camp in Cheung Chau, with Huo En-de and Chao Chun-ying as speakers. Over 80 people attended, and on the final night, many young participants were deeply moved, confessing their sins and praying throughout the night. After this spiritual revival, the youths experienced significant changes in their lives, emphasizing:

                                                1. The doctrine of rebirth.

                                                  1. Pursuing holy living.

                                                    1. Studying and learning the Word of God through the Bible.

                                                      1. Actively spreading the gospel.

                                                        1. Enthusiastically serving in the church.

                                                          However, these changes led to dissatisfaction with the existing Baptist church structure, and church leaders found the youth increasingly ‚Äúdisobedient.‚Äù This tension ultimately resulted in the separation of the evangelical movement from Baptist churches in Caine Road, Kowloon City, and Shau Kei Wan during the 1960s.

                                                          ‚Äî

                                                          1. Dialogue Between Christianity and Chinese Religious Culture (Ying 2015)

                                                            In 1957, the Christian Study Centre on Chinese Religion and Culture (later renamed the Christian Cultural Study Centre) was established as an interdenominational organization with a mission to promote indigenization. The center‚Äôs founding was closely related to the setbacks faced by Christian missionary efforts in China during the 1950s. Some missionaries in Hong Kong recognized that one of the greatest lessons from the history of Christianity in China was its failure to understand traditional Chinese culture and religion.

                                                            Under the leadership of Bishop R. O. Hall, a group of missionaries initiated the establishment of the center to reflect on these issues. Bishop Hall argued that a significant flaw in Western Christianity was its separation of religious faith from culture. Among the three million Chinese people in Hong Kong, most adhered to traditional cultural and religious practices. Chinese Christians often lost connection with their country‚Äôs traditional way of life after conversion. Thus, the center‚Äôs mission was to study God‚Äôs self-revelation among the Chinese people and their responses to Him, aiming to assist those spreading the gospel in China.

                                                            The World Council of Churches also sought to establish regional research centers worldwide to study Christianity in different cultural contexts and reflect on the church‚Äôs relationship with the world. With funding from the World Council of Churches and the International Missionary Council, the Hong Kong center focused on studying contemporary non-Christian beliefs in China, understanding their vitality, and relating these findings to the practical life of Chinese churches. Activities included publishing the English quarterly Quarterly Notes, the Chinese magazine Ching Fung, and organizing research meetings and seminars.

                                                            Key participants included Western missionaries like Paul Jefferies (Methodist Church), Andrew T. Roy (American Presbyterian Mission), R. P. Kramers (Netherlands Missionary Council), Loren Noren (American Baptist), and Gerhard Reichlt (Tao Fong Shan). Chinese contributors included Li Siu-keung, Hu Zan-yun, Zhou Yi-fu, Hsieh Fu-ya, Ho Shi-ming, Kung Tin-man, and Fung Shang-lee. Through their collaborative efforts, ‚Äúindigenization‚Äù or ‚Äúcontextual theology‚Äù became a central focus of the center during the 1950s and 1960s.

    Geopolitical Factors Influencing Hong Kong in the 1950s

    The new geopolitical landscape in East Asia during the Cold War profoundly impacted Hong Kong in the 1950s. This period was shaped by two major events: the division between the newly established Chinese Communist regime on the mainland and the Kuomintang (KMT) government in Taiwan following the end of the Chinese Civil War, and the outbreak of the Korean War. These two developments were interrelated, significantly influencing Hong Kong‚Äôs trajectory (Mark 2004; Roberts & Carroll 2016).

    With the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, a massive influx of Chinese refugees poured into Hong Kong. By 1950, Hong Kong‚Äôs population reached 2.36 million and rose to 2.5 million by 1956, with an estimated one million being refugees from China. In 1955, the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR) published The Problem of Chinese Refugees in Hong Kong, providing a comprehensive account of the situation and the challenges faced by Chinese refugees. The 1956 Hong Kong government annual report identified education, healthcare, housing, public security, and sanitation as the main social issues resulting from the surge in population (Hing 2002: 33-44; Hing 2004: 81-87).

    The PRC‚Äôs Policy on Hong Kong and Strategic Utilization

    After the founding of the PRC, the colonial future of Hong Kong attracted close attention. Many missionary societies speculated that the Chinese Communist Party (CCP), driven by nationalist considerations, might ‚Äúliberate‚Äù Hong Kong. To avoid potential losses, these societies were hesitant to invest resources in the territory. However, in 1954, Premier Zhou Enlai clarified China‚Äôs policy on Hong Kong, stating that ‚Äúthe time was not yet ripe‚Äù to reclaim it.

    During the Korean War, the CCP‚Äôs involvement led to a UN embargo on strategic goods and a complete U.S. trade embargo on China. Hong Kong emerged as a vital hub for smuggling goods into China and facilitating remittance flows from overseas Chinese. In 1957, Zhou reaffirmed Hong Kong‚Äôs role as an economic bridge between China and the outside world. Under the policy of ‚Äúlong-term planning and full utilization,‚Äù Hong Kong retained its colonial status and assumed a unique role amid the Cold War, earning the nickname ‚Äúthe Berlin of the East‚Äù (Carroll 2007: 140-148).

    Balancing Between the CCP and KMT

    Hong Kong sought to maintain a delicate balance in the face of the rivalry between the CCP and KMT. Although the British government was allied with the United States, it avoided turning Hong Kong into an anti-communist stronghold. Measures to limit CCP activities in Hong Kong were also applied to pro-KMT forces. Britain aimed to preserve Hong Kong‚Äôs status as a free-trade port without entangling it in the political struggle between the two parties.

    Nevertheless, this neutrality could not prevent the presence of CCP and KMT influence. Political factions emerged in industries, media, cultural institutions, neighborhood organizations, unions, chambers of commerce, and schools, divided into pro-KMT ‚Äúrightists‚Äù and pro-CCP ‚Äúleftists.‚Äù Despite efforts to prevent political propaganda in schools, some institutions still aligned with either the KMT or CCP. Meanwhile, the U.S. sought to strengthen anti-communist propaganda, promoting democracy and freedom among Chinese refugees. During this period, Hong Kong became an important cultural Cold War battleground and a middle ground for CCP and KMT forces (Lo 2020: 29-40, 98; Lui 2014: 1-12).

    The colonial government‚Äôs support for Christian schools in the 1950s and 1960s was partly motivated by a desire to counter communist ideology (Leung & Chan 2003: 23-28).

    Refugees and Missionary Efforts

    The influx of Chinese refugees reinforced Hong Kong‚Äôs character as a migrant-refugee society. Pro-CCP factions praised the ‚ÄúRed Motherland,‚Äù while pro-KMT factions condemned the CCP as ‚ÄúRed Bandits.‚Äù Some ‚ÄúSouthbound Literati,‚Äù nostalgic for ‚Äúcultural China,‚Äù created diverse imaginations of China in 1950s Hong Kong.

    Among the refugee influx were Western missionaries fleeing China. Some initially attempted to remain under the communist regime. In June 1950, 1,900 Protestant missionaries were still in China, a significant decrease from over 4,000 after the war. However, uncertain prospects forced many to leave. By July 1950, the number of Protestant missionaries had dropped to 1,200. As China‚Äôs involvement in the Korean War escalated, the CCP launched the ‚ÄúResist America, Aid Korea‚Äù campaign, which heightened anti-American sentiment and targeted missionaries. In February 1951, even the China Inland Mission (CIM), which had long resisted withdrawal, decided to fully evacuate its personnel. Other missionary organizations followed suit, demanding complete withdrawal of missionaries from China (Hing 2012: 262-281).

    Concurrently, the CCP launched the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform Movement,‚Äù aimed at creating self-governing, self-supporting, and self-propagating churches while severing ties with imperialism and purging ‚ÄúAmerican imperialist toxins.‚Äù This accelerated the CCP‚Äôs control over and transformation of Chinese churches (Hing 2012: 21-76).

    Hong Kong as a Refugee Hub and Missionary Base

    Amid the ‚ÄúGreat Exodus,‚Äù British Hong Kong, located on the southern frontier of China, became the first stop for departing missionaries. Many missionaries, witnessing the plight of refugees in Hong Kong, began working with them even without approval from their missionary societies. Rennie‚Äôs Mill (Tiu Keng Leng) became the largest refugee camp, where missionaries from organizations like the Norwegian Lutheran Mission, Lutheran Church‚ÄìMissouri Synod, Christian and Missionary Alliance, American Evangelical and Reformed Church, Swedish Free Mission, and Mission Covenant Church of Norway served. These missionaries established churches, schools, medical services, relief programs, and seminaries within the refugee camps (Leung 2000; Yang 2014).

    Under the guise of aiding Chinese refugees, significant missionary resources poured into Hong Kong.

    Èö®ËëóÊñ∞‰∏≠ÂúãÊîøÊ¨äÂª∫Á´ãÔºåÂ§ßÈáè‰∏≠ÂúãÈõ£Ê∞ëÊπßËá≥È¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ1950Âπ¥È¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∫∫Âè£ÈÅî236Ëê¨Ôºå1956Âπ¥Êõ¥Â¢ûËá≥250Ëê¨Ôºå‰º∞Ë®àÂÖ∂‰∏≠100Ëê¨ÊòØ‰æÜËá™‰∏≠ÂúãÁöÑÈõ£Ê∞ë„ÄÇ1955Âπ¥ÔºåËÅØÂêàÂúãÈõ£Ê∞ëÈ´òÁ¥öÂ∞àÂì°ÂÖ¨ÁΩ≤Âá∫Áâà„ÄäÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∏≠ÂúãÈõ£Ê∞ëÂïèÈ°å„ÄãÔºàThe Problem of Chinese Refugees in Hong KongÔºâÔºåÂ∞çÂú®Ê∏Ø‰∏≠ÂúãÈõ£Ê∞ëÁöÑÊÉÖÊ≥ÅÂèäÂÖ∂Èù¢Â∞çÁöÑÂïèÈ°å‰ΩúÂÖ®Èù¢Êï¥ÁêÜ„ÄÇ1956Âπ¥ÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊîøÂ∫úÂú®Âπ¥Â†±‰∏≠ÔºåÊèêÂèäÁï∂ÂâçÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ§æÊúÉÁöÑ‰∏ªË¶ÅÂïèÈ°åÔºåÂ∞±ÊòØÂõ†ÊáâÁßªÂÖ•‰∫∫Âè£ÊøÄÂ¢ûËÄåË°çÁîüÁöÑÊïôËÇ≤„ÄÅÈÜ´ÁôÇ„ÄÅÊàøÂ±ã„ÄÅÊ≤ªÂÆâ„ÄÅË°õÁîüÁ≠âÂïèÈ°åÔºàÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û2002:33-44Ôºå2004: 81-87Ôºâ„ÄÇ

    ‰∏çÈÅéÔºåÊñ∞‰∏≠ÂúãÊîøÊ¨äÂª∫Á´ãÂæåÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÈÄôÊÆñÊ∞ëÂú∞ÁöÑÂâçÊôØ‰πüÂèóÂà∞ÂØÜÂàáÈóúÊ≥®„ÄÇÂ∞çÊ≠§Ôºå‰∏çÂ∞ëÂ∑ÆÊúÉË©ï‰º∞‰∏≠ÂÖ±Âü∫ÊñºÊ∞ëÊóè‰∏ªÁæ©ËÄÉÈáèÔºåÊúÉÂçó‰∏ã„ÄåËß£Êîæ„ÄçÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄÇÂõ†Ê≠§Ôºå‰ªñÂÄë‰∏çÈ°òÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÊäïÊîæË≥áÊ∫êÔºå ‰ª•ÂÖçÂÜç‰∏ÄÊ¨°ÊãõÂèóÊêçÂ§±„ÄÇ1954Âπ¥Ôºå‰∏≠ÂÖ±È†òÂ∞é‰∫∫Âë®ÊÅ©‰æÜÈó°Ëø∞Â∞çÊ∏ØÊîøÁ≠ñÔºåË°®Á§∫„ÄåÊôÇÊ©üÂ∞öÊú™ÊàêÁÜüÔºåÊàëÂÄë‰∏çË¶ÅÂéªË´á„ÄçÊî∂ÂõûÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ‰∫ãÂØ¶‰∏äÔºåÁî±Êñº‰∏≠ÂÖ±‰ªãÂÖ•ÈüìÊà∞ÂèóËÅØÂêàÂúãÂØ¶ÊñΩÊà∞Áï•Áâ©Ë≥áÁ¶ÅÈÅãÔºåÁæéÂúã‰πüÂ∞ç‰∏≠ÂúãÂØ¶Ë°åÂÖ®Èù¢Ë≤øÊòìÁ¶ÅÈÅãÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊàêÁÇ∫Á¶ÅÈÅãÊúüÈñìÂ∞áË≤®ÂìÅËµ∞ÁßÅÈÄ≤‰∏≠ÂúãÁöÑÈáçË¶ÅÂú∞ÊñπÔºåÂÉëÂåØ‰πüËóâÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂæó‰ª•ÂåØÂõû‰∏≠Âúã„ÄÇ1957Âπ¥ÔºåÂë®ÊÅ©‰æÜÂÜçËÇØÂÆöÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊàêÁÇ∫‰∏≠ÂúãËàáÂúãÂ§ñÈÄ≤Ë°åÁ∂ìÊøüËÅØÁπ´ÁöÑÂ•ΩËôï„ÄÇÂú®„ÄåÈï∑ÊúüÊâìÁÆóÔºåÂÖÖÂàÜÂà©Áî®„ÄçÁöÑÊñπÈáù‰∏ãÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂæó‰ª•‰øùÊåÅÊÆñÊ∞ëÂú∞Âú∞‰ΩçÔºå‰∏¶Âú®Êù±Ë•øÂÜ∑Êà∞Ê†ºÂ±Ä‰∏≠ÔºåÁ∂≠ÊåÅÁç®ÁâπËßíËâ≤Ôºå‰∏¶Êúâ„ÄåÊù±ÊñπÊüèÊûó„ÄçÁöÑÁ®±ËôüÔºàCarroll 2007:140-148Ôºâ„ÄÇ

    ÂêåÊôÇÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂú®ÂúãÂÖ±ÂÖ©Â≤∏Â∞çÁ´ãÁöÑÊ†ºÂ±Ä‰∏≠Ôºå‰πüËá¥ÂäõÁ∂≠ÊåÅÂæÆÂ¶ôÁöÑÂπ≥Ë°°Èóú‰øÇ„ÄÇËã±ÂúãÊîøÂ∫úÈõñÁÑ∂ÊòØÁæéÂúãÁõüÂèãÔºå‰ΩÜÂçª‰∏çÂ∏åÊúõÂ∞áÈ¶ôÊ∏ØËÆäÊàêÂèçÂÖ±ÂâçÁ∑ö„ÄÇÁï∂Â±ÄÈôêÂà∂ÂÖ±Áî¢Èª®Âú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÊ¥ªÂãïÁöÑÊé™ÊñΩÔºåÂêåÊ®£‰πüÁî®Âú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÁöÑË¶™ÂúãÊ∞ëÈª®Âã¢ÂäõË∫´‰∏ä„ÄÇËã±ÂúãÂ∏åÊúõÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÁ∂≠ÊåÅËá™Áî±Ë≤øÊòìÁöÑÂïÜÂü†ÔºåËÄå‰∏çÊç≤ÂÖ•ÂúãÂÖ±ÁöÑÊîøÊ≤ªÈ¨•Áà≠‰πã‰∏≠„ÄÇ‰∏çÈÅéÔºåÈÄôÁ®Æ‰∏≠Á´ãÊîøÁ≠ñÔºå‰ªçÁÑ°Ê≥ïÈòªÂäõÂúãÂÖ±Âã¢ÂäõÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÁöÑÊ¥ªÂãï„ÄÇÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÂú®‰∏çÂêåË°åÊ•≠ÁöÑ‰ºÅÊ•≠ÂñÆ‰Ωç„ÄÅÂ†±Âàä„ÄÅÊñáÂåñÊ©üÊßã„ÄÅË°óÂùäÁµÑÁπî„ÄÅÂ∑•ÊúÉ„ÄÅÂïÜÊúÉÂèäÂ≠∏Ê†°Á≠âÔºåÂàÜÂà•Âá∫ÁèæË¶™ÂúãÊ∞ëÈª®ÁöÑ„ÄåÂè≥„ÄçÊ¥æÂèäË¶™ÂÖ±ÁöÑ„ÄåÂ∑¶„ÄçÊ¥æ„ÄÇÂú®Â≠∏Ê†°ÊïôËÇ≤ÊñπÈù¢ÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊÆñÊ∞ëÂú∞ÊîøÂ∫úÈõñ‰∏ÄÁõ¥Èò≤ÁØÑÊïôËÇ≤ÊàêÁÇ∫ÊîøÊ≤ªÂã¢ÂäõÂÆ£ÂÇ≥ÂèäÊª≤ÈÄèÂ∑•ÂÖ∑Ôºå‰ΩÜ‰ªçÊúâÈÉ®ÂàÜÂ≠∏Ê†°ÂÖ∑Ë¶™ÂúãÊ∞ëÈª®ÂèäÂÖ±Áî¢Èª®ÁöÑÁ´ãÂ†¥„ÄÇËÄåÈáùÂ∞çÈÄÉÈÅøÂÖ±Áî¢ÊîøÊ¨ä‰æÜÊ∏ØÁöÑ‰∏≠ÂúãÈõ£Ê∞ëÔºåÁæéÂúã‰πü‰ºÅÂúñÂä†Âº∑ÂÆ£ÊèöÊ∞ë‰∏ªËá™Áî±ÁöÑÂèçÂÖ±ÂÆ£ÂÇ≥„ÄÇ‰∫îÂçÅÂπ¥‰ª£ÔºåÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÊàêÁÇ∫ÊñáÂåñÂÜ∑Êà∞ÁöÑÈáçË¶ÅÊà∞Â†¥ÔºåÂêåÊôÇ‰πüÊòØÂúãÂÖ±Âã¢Âäõ‰∏¶Â≠òÁöÑ‰∏≠ÈñìÂú∞Â∏∂ÔºàÁæÖÊ∞∏Áîü2020Ôºö29-40, 98ÔºõÂëÇÂ§ßÊ®Ç2014Ôºö1-12Ôºâ„ÄÇ‰∫îÂçÅËá≥ÂÖ≠ÂçÅÂπ¥‰ª£ÔºåÊÆñÊ∞ëÂú∞ÊîøÂ∫úÊîØÊåÅÂü∫Áù£ÂÆóÊïôËæ¶Â≠∏Ôºå‰∫ãÂØ¶‰∏ä‰πüÊúâÂú®ÊÑèË≠òÂΩ¢ÊÖã‰∏äÊäóË°°ÂÖ±Áî¢‰∏ªÁæ©ÁöÑËÄÉÈáèÔºàLeung & Chan 2003: 23-28Ôºâ„ÄÇ

Hong Kong

Historical Period: Before 1960 (focus on the decade from 1950 to 1959. As background, please briefly mention significant regulations and events from earlier periods, especially those that had a major impact on the circumstances of this decade.)

        In the 1950s, the Legislative Council passed incorporation ordinances related to Christian organizations, including:

        • Kowloon Tong Church of the Chinese Christian and Missionary Alliance Incorporation Ordinance, 1950

          • Norwegian Seamen‚Äôs Mission Incorporation Ordinance, 1950

            • Chinese Rhenish Church, Hong Kong Synod, Incorporation Ordinance, 1951

              • Salvation Army Incorporation Ordinance, 1951

                • Church of Christ in China, Wanchai Church, Incorporation Ordinance, 1952

                  • Pentecostal Holiness Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1952

                    • Emmanuel Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1954

                      • Tsimshatsui Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1954

                        • Hong Kong Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                          • Hong Kong Christian Council Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                            • Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission Incorporation Ordinance, 1955

                              • Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance, 1956

                                • Kowloon City Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance, 1957

                                  • Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance, 1958

                                    The incorporation ordinances granted legal status to Christian churches and organizations, particularly addressing issues related to the ownership and disposal of land. Churches or organizations incorporated in the 1950s can be categorized into three groups:

                                    1. Churches adapting to the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù movement in China:

                                      After the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China in 1949, Chinese Christianity began to follow the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù path to sever ties with imperialism. Denominations that had been active in Hong Kong since the 19th century, whose organizational structures were part of South China or Guangdong, faced the need to establish legal independence based in Hong Kong due to the separation from their counterparts in Mainland China. Examples include the Chinese Rhenish Church, Hong Kong Synod, Incorporation Ordinance (1951), Tsung Tsin Mission of Hong Kong Incorporation Ordinance (1956), and Hong Kong Council of the Church of Christ in China Incorporation Ordinance (1958).

                                      1. Local churches established before 1950:

                                        Examples include the Kowloon Tong Church of the Chinese Christian and Missionary Alliance Incorporation Ordinance (1950), Norwegian Seamen‚Äôs Mission Incorporation Ordinance (1950), Church of Christ in China, Wanchai Church, Incorporation Ordinance (1952), Pentecostal Holiness Church Incorporation Ordinance (1952), Emmanuel Church Incorporation Ordinance (1954), Tsimshatsui Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1954), Hong Kong Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1955), and Kowloon City Baptist Church Incorporation Ordinance (1957).

                                        1. Newly established organizations after 1950:

                                          Examples include the Hong Kong Christian Council Incorporation Ordinance (1955) and the Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission Incorporation Ordinance (1955).

                                          1. Criticism of Evangelist Chao Shih-kuang by Leftist Newspapers

                                            In the 1950s, there were no significant incidents of hostility toward Christianity in Hong Kong. However, individual Christian figures were targeted, particularly evangelist Chao Shih-kuang, who arrived in Hong Kong from Shanghai in 1949. Chao had founded the Ling Liang World-Wide Evangelistic Mission in Shanghai in 1946. Following the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China on October 1, 1949, he and his family left Shanghai, traveling via Zhoushan to Taipei and later flying to Hong Kong. In 1951, as part of the Chinese Communist Party‚Äôs ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform‚Äù movement, denunciation meetings were held to criticize the ties between Christianity and imperialism. During a denunciation meeting at Shanghai‚Äôs Ling Liang Church in May, Chao became the primary target (Hing 2012: 98-99).

                                            On June 10, 1951, the leftist Hong Kong newspaper Ta Kung Pao published a front-page article titled ‚ÄúChao Shih-kuang, Who Fled from Shanghai to Hong Kong, Slanders the Motherland‚Äù. The report detailed the accusations made against Chao in Shanghai, claiming that he was spreading ‚Äúabsurd and reactionary propaganda‚Äù in Hong Kong and accusing him of slandering the patriotic Three-Self Reform movement of Chinese Christians (Ta Kung Pao 1951a). Other local newspapers also criticized Chao for his close ties with the Kuomintang government (Hsu 1966: 135-136). Concerned for his safety, Chao and his family moved to Indonesia in July 1952 and later relocated to the United States (Hing 2004: 116-117).

                                            During the Chinese church‚Äôs denunciation campaigns in 1951-1952, Hong Kong‚Äôs leftist newspapers also reported on related events. For instance, in April 1951, Ta Kung Pao reported on the accusations against missionaries such as Francis W. Price in Shanghai (Ta Kung Pao 1951b). In June, it covered the Shanghai Christian denunciation meeting, during which the Methodist Church expelled Chiang Kai-shek and Soong Mei-ling from their membership (Ta Kung Pao 1951c).

                                            ‚Äî

                                            1. Controversy Over Renting Theaters for Worship by Southbound Evangelists

                                              Within the Christian community, debates arose over innovative evangelistic methods. In the early 1950s, evangelists arriving from China, such as Ji Chih-wen and Chao Shih-kuang, rented theaters for gatherings and evangelism. For example, Ji Chih-wen (China Evangelistic Mission) held Mandarin-language meetings at Jordan‚Äôs Happy Theatre, attracting nearly 1,000 attendees each time. Similarly, Chao conducted Sunday worship services at Central‚Äôs Entertainment Theatre, filling all 1,200 seats.

                                              This novel approach of using theaters for religious gatherings was not well-received by Hong Kong‚Äôs mainstream churches. In February 1950, the Hong Kong Christian Council issued a notice titled ‚ÄúAdvice to Believers to Attend Meetings Cautiously‚Äù. The notice criticized the use of theaters for Sunday worship, which included rituals such as responsive readings, choir performances, and offerings, akin to formal church services. The council argued that such gatherings, which should promote reverence and solemnity, were inappropriate for theaters, which were seen as ‚Äúworldly‚Äù venues unsuitable for ‚Äúsacred‚Äù religious worship (Hong Kong Christian Council 1950: 6-7). This controversy highlighted the tension between the innovative methods of newly arrived evangelists and the traditional practices of the established church, which resisted the use of secular spaces for sacred purposes.

                                              ‚Äî

                                              1. Division in the Baptist Youth Fellowship (Hing 2004: 166-167)

                                                The Baptist Youth Fellowship Union was a joint organization of various Baptist youth fellowships in Hong Kong, with officers elected by the fellowships. The union operated independently, not under the Baptist Convention or any specific Baptist church. Seasonal meetings were held quarterly, and annual summer camps attracted 300-400 participants, primarily from the Caine Road, Tsim Sha Tsui, and Kowloon City churches.

                                                In 1951, the union held a summer camp in Cheung Chau, with Huo En-de and Chao Chun-ying as speakers. Over 80 people attended, and on the final night, many young participants were deeply moved, confessing their sins and praying throughout the night. After this spiritual revival, the youths experienced significant changes in their lives, emphasizing:

                                                1. The doctrine of rebirth.

                                                  1. Pursuing holy living.

                                                    1. Studying and learning the Word of God through the Bible.

                                                      1. Actively spreading the gospel.

                                                        1. Enthusiastically serving in the church.

                                                          However, these changes led to dissatisfaction with the existing Baptist church structure, and church leaders found the youth increasingly ‚Äúdisobedient.‚Äù This tension ultimately resulted in the separation of the evangelical movement from Baptist churches in Caine Road, Kowloon City, and Shau Kei Wan during the 1960s.

                                                          ‚Äî

                                                          1. Dialogue Between Christianity and Chinese Religious Culture (Ying 2015)

                                                            In 1957, the Christian Study Centre on Chinese Religion and Culture (later renamed the Christian Cultural Study Centre) was established as an interdenominational organization with a mission to promote indigenization. The center‚Äôs founding was closely related to the setbacks faced by Christian missionary efforts in China during the 1950s. Some missionaries in Hong Kong recognized that one of the greatest lessons from the history of Christianity in China was its failure to understand traditional Chinese culture and religion.

                                                            Under the leadership of Bishop R. O. Hall, a group of missionaries initiated the establishment of the center to reflect on these issues. Bishop Hall argued that a significant flaw in Western Christianity was its separation of religious faith from culture. Among the three million Chinese people in Hong Kong, most adhered to traditional cultural and religious practices. Chinese Christians often lost connection with their country‚Äôs traditional way of life after conversion. Thus, the center‚Äôs mission was to study God‚Äôs self-revelation among the Chinese people and their responses to Him, aiming to assist those spreading the gospel in China.

                                                            The World Council of Churches also sought to establish regional research centers worldwide to study Christianity in different cultural contexts and reflect on the church‚Äôs relationship with the world. With funding from the World Council of Churches and the International Missionary Council, the Hong Kong center focused on studying contemporary non-Christian beliefs in China, understanding their vitality, and relating these findings to the practical life of Chinese churches. Activities included publishing the English quarterly Quarterly Notes, the Chinese magazine Ching Fung, and organizing research meetings and seminars.

                                                            Key participants included Western missionaries like Paul Jefferies (Methodist Church), Andrew T. Roy (American Presbyterian Mission), R. P. Kramers (Netherlands Missionary Council), Loren Noren (American Baptist), and Gerhard Reichlt (Tao Fong Shan). Chinese contributors included Li Siu-keung, Hu Zan-yun, Zhou Yi-fu, Hsieh Fu-ya, Ho Shi-ming, Kung Tin-man, and Fung Shang-lee. Through their collaborative efforts, ‚Äúindigenization‚Äù or ‚Äúcontextual theology‚Äù became a central focus of the center during the 1950s and 1960s.

    Geopolitical Factors Influencing Hong Kong in the 1950s

    The new geopolitical landscape in East Asia during the Cold War profoundly impacted Hong Kong in the 1950s. This period was shaped by two major events: the division between the newly established Chinese Communist regime on the mainland and the Kuomintang (KMT) government in Taiwan following the end of the Chinese Civil War, and the outbreak of the Korean War. These two developments were interrelated, significantly influencing Hong Kong‚Äôs trajectory (Mark 2004; Roberts & Carroll 2016).

    With the establishment of the People‚Äôs Republic of China, a massive influx of Chinese refugees poured into Hong Kong. By 1950, Hong Kong‚Äôs population reached 2.36 million and rose to 2.5 million by 1956, with an estimated one million being refugees from China. In 1955, the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR) published The Problem of Chinese Refugees in Hong Kong, providing a comprehensive account of the situation and the challenges faced by Chinese refugees. The 1956 Hong Kong government annual report identified education, healthcare, housing, public security, and sanitation as the main social issues resulting from the surge in population (Hing 2002: 33-44; Hing 2004: 81-87).

    The PRC‚Äôs Policy on Hong Kong and Strategic Utilization

    After the founding of the PRC, the colonial future of Hong Kong attracted close attention. Many missionary societies speculated that the Chinese Communist Party (CCP), driven by nationalist considerations, might ‚Äúliberate‚Äù Hong Kong. To avoid potential losses, these societies were hesitant to invest resources in the territory. However, in 1954, Premier Zhou Enlai clarified China‚Äôs policy on Hong Kong, stating that ‚Äúthe time was not yet ripe‚Äù to reclaim it.

    During the Korean War, the CCP‚Äôs involvement led to a UN embargo on strategic goods and a complete U.S. trade embargo on China. Hong Kong emerged as a vital hub for smuggling goods into China and facilitating remittance flows from overseas Chinese. In 1957, Zhou reaffirmed Hong Kong‚Äôs role as an economic bridge between China and the outside world. Under the policy of ‚Äúlong-term planning and full utilization,‚Äù Hong Kong retained its colonial status and assumed a unique role amid the Cold War, earning the nickname ‚Äúthe Berlin of the East‚Äù (Carroll 2007: 140-148).

    Balancing Between the CCP and KMT

    Hong Kong sought to maintain a delicate balance in the face of the rivalry between the CCP and KMT. Although the British government was allied with the United States, it avoided turning Hong Kong into an anti-communist stronghold. Measures to limit CCP activities in Hong Kong were also applied to pro-KMT forces. Britain aimed to preserve Hong Kong‚Äôs status as a free-trade port without entangling it in the political struggle between the two parties.

    Nevertheless, this neutrality could not prevent the presence of CCP and KMT influence. Political factions emerged in industries, media, cultural institutions, neighborhood organizations, unions, chambers of commerce, and schools, divided into pro-KMT ‚Äúrightists‚Äù and pro-CCP ‚Äúleftists.‚Äù Despite efforts to prevent political propaganda in schools, some institutions still aligned with either the KMT or CCP. Meanwhile, the U.S. sought to strengthen anti-communist propaganda, promoting democracy and freedom among Chinese refugees. During this period, Hong Kong became an important cultural Cold War battleground and a middle ground for CCP and KMT forces (Lo 2020: 29-40, 98; Lui 2014: 1-12).

    The colonial government‚Äôs support for Christian schools in the 1950s and 1960s was partly motivated by a desire to counter communist ideology (Leung & Chan 2003: 23-28).

    Refugees and Missionary Efforts

    The influx of Chinese refugees reinforced Hong Kong‚Äôs character as a migrant-refugee society. Pro-CCP factions praised the ‚ÄúRed Motherland,‚Äù while pro-KMT factions condemned the CCP as ‚ÄúRed Bandits.‚Äù Some ‚ÄúSouthbound Literati,‚Äù nostalgic for ‚Äúcultural China,‚Äù created diverse imaginations of China in 1950s Hong Kong.

    Among the refugee influx were Western missionaries fleeing China. Some initially attempted to remain under the communist regime. In June 1950, 1,900 Protestant missionaries were still in China, a significant decrease from over 4,000 after the war. However, uncertain prospects forced many to leave. By July 1950, the number of Protestant missionaries had dropped to 1,200. As China‚Äôs involvement in the Korean War escalated, the CCP launched the ‚ÄúResist America, Aid Korea‚Äù campaign, which heightened anti-American sentiment and targeted missionaries. In February 1951, even the China Inland Mission (CIM), which had long resisted withdrawal, decided to fully evacuate its personnel. Other missionary organizations followed suit, demanding complete withdrawal of missionaries from China (Hing 2012: 262-281).

    Concurrently, the CCP launched the ‚ÄúThree-Self Reform Movement,‚Äù aimed at creating self-governing, self-supporting, and self-propagating churches while severing ties with imperialism and purging ‚ÄúAmerican imperialist toxins.‚Äù This accelerated the CCP‚Äôs control over and transformation of Chinese churches (Hing 2012: 21-76).

    Hong Kong as a Refugee Hub and Missionary Base

    Amid the ‚ÄúGreat Exodus,‚Äù British Hong Kong, located on the southern frontier of China, became the first stop for departing missionaries. Many missionaries, witnessing the plight of refugees in Hong Kong, began working with them even without approval from their missionary societies. Rennie‚Äôs Mill (Tiu Keng Leng) became the largest refugee camp, where missionaries from organizations like the Norwegian Lutheran Mission, Lutheran Church‚ÄìMissouri Synod, Christian and Missionary Alliance, American Evangelical and Reformed Church, Swedish Free Mission, and Mission Covenant Church of Norway served. These missionaries established churches, schools, medical services, relief programs, and seminaries within the refugee camps (Leung 2000; Yang 2014).

    Under the guise of aiding Chinese refugees, significant missionary resources poured into Hong Kong.
